</ol>
<ul>
<li>apparent indifference to pain/temperature</li>
<li>adverse response to specific sounds or textures</li>
<li>excessive smelling or touching of objects</li>
<li>visual fascination with lights or movement</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-5-0-depression">2.5.0 Depression<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-5-0-depression" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="2-5-1-purpose-of-the-unit">2.5.1 Purpose of the Unit<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-5-1-purpose-of-the-unit" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>The purpose of this unit is to equip healthcare workers with knowledge, skills and attitudes to enable them create awareness, identify, demystify and assist in management, effective referral and follow up of Depression and Bipolar Disorders.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="2-5-1-definition-of-depression">2.5.1 Definition of Depression<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-5-1-definition-of-depression" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Depression is a mood disorder that causes a persistent feeling of sadness and loss of interest. Also called major depressive disorder or clinical depression, it affects feelings, thinking, behaviour, and can lead to a variety of emotional and physical problems. There may be trouble doing normal day-to-day activities, and sometimes feeling as if life is not worth living.</p>
<p>Depression affects people of all races and socioeconomic class and can occur at any time. Depression is not same as sadness and grief caused by a challenging life experience such as death of a loved one. Although depression may occur only once during your life, people typically have multiple episodes and these episodes, symptoms occur most of the day, nearly every day.</p>
<p>The real causes of depression are unknown. However, there are factors thought to contribute to depression.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="2-5-2-causes-of-depression">2.5.2 Causes of Depression<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-5-2-causes-of-depression" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>It is not known exactly what causes depression. As with many mental disorders, a variety of factors may be involved, such as:</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>a. Biological differences. People with depression appear to have physical changes in their brains. The significance of these changes is still uncertain, but may eventually help pinpoint causes.</p>
<p>b. Brain chemistry. Neurotransmitters are naturally occurring brain chemicals that likely play a role in depression. Recent research indicates that changes in the function and effect of these neurotransmitters and how they interact with neurocircuits involved in maintaining mood stability may play a significant role in depression and its treatment.</p>
<p>c. Hormones. Changes in the body's balance of hormones may be involved in causing or triggering depression. Hormone changes can result with pregnancy and during the weeks or months after delivery (postpartum) and from thyroid problems, menopause or a number of other conditions.</p>
<p>d. Inherited traits. Depression is more common in people whose blood relatives also have this condition. Researchers are trying to find genes that may be involved in causing depression.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-5-3-risk-factors-to-depression">2.5.3 Risk factors to Depression<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-5-3-risk-factors-to-depression" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Depression often begins in the teens, 20s or 30s, but it can happen at any age. More women than men are diagnosed with depression, but this may be due in part because women are more likely to seek treatment.</p>
<p>Factors that seem to increase the risk of developing or triggering depression include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Certain personality traits, such as low self-esteem and being too dependent, self-critical or pessimistic</li>
<li>Traumatic or stressful events, such as physical or sexual abuse, the death or loss of a loved one, a difficult relationship, or financial problems</li>
<li>Being lesbian, gay, bisexual or transgender, or having variations in the development of genital organs that aren't clearly male or female (intersex) in an unsupportive situation</li>
<li>History of other mental health disorders, such as anxiety disorder, bipolar disorder, substance use disorders, eating disorders or post-traumatic stress disorder</li>
<li>Serious or chronic illness, including cancer, stroke, chronic pain or heart disease</li>
</ul>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>5</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Certain medications, such as some high blood pressure medications or sleeping pills (talk to your doctor before stopping any medication)</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-5-4-common-signs-and-symptoms-of-depression">2.5.4 Common signs and symptoms of depression<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-5-4-common-signs-and-symptoms-of-depression" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The signs and symptoms of depression occur at various different levels as follows:</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="a-emotional-level">a) Emotional level<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#a-emotional-level" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Continuous low mood</li>
<li>Loss of interest or pleasure</li>
<li>Hopelessness</li>
<li>Helplessness</li>
<li>Worthlessness/low self-esteem</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="b-psychological-level">b) Psychological level<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#b-psychological-level" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Guilty/negative attitude to self</li>
<li>Loss of concentration/memory</li>
<li>Persistent thoughts of death or suicide</li>
<li>Tearfulness</li>
<li>Extremely sensitive to criticism</li>
<li>Negative thoughts about everything</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="c-physical-level">c) Physical level<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#c-physical-level" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Slowing down in activities or agitation</li>
<li>Tiredness/lack of energy</li>
<li>Sleep problems (total lack of sleep, difficulty in staying asleep or excessive sleep)</li>
<li>Disturbed appetite (Increased or decreased)</li>
<li>Loss or increase of weight</li>
<li>Unkempt</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-5-5-management-of-depression">2.5.5 Management of Depression<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-5-5-management-of-depression" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Medications and psychotherapy are effective for most people with depression. A primary care/ doctor or psychiatrist can prescribe medications to relieve symptoms. However, many people with depression also benefit from seeing a psychiatrist, psychologist or other mental health professional.</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="psychotherapy">Psychotherapy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#psychotherapy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Psychotherapy is a general term for treating depression by talking about your condition and related issues with a mental health professional. Psychotherapy is also known as talk therapy or psychological therapy.</p>
<p>Different types of psychotherapy can be effective for depression, such as cognitive behavioral therapy or interpersonal therapy. Your mental health professional may also recommend other types of therapies. Psychotherapy can help you:</p>
<ul>
<li>Adjust to a crisis or other current difficulty</li>
<li>Identify negative beliefs and behaviors and replace them with healthy, positive ones</li>
<li>Explore relationships and experiences, and develop positive interactions with others</li>
<li>Find better ways to cope and solve problems</li>
<li>Identify issues that contribute to your depression and change behaviors that make it worse</li>
<li>Regain a sense of satisfaction and control in your life and help ease depression symptoms, such as hopelessness and anger</li>
<li>Learn to set realistic goals for your life</li>
<li>Develop the ability to tolerate and accept distress using healthier behaviors</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-5-6-prevention-of-depression">2.5.6 Prevention of Depression<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-5-6-prevention-of-depression" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>There is no sure way to prevent depression. However, these strategies may help:</p>
<ul>
<li>Take steps to control stress, to increase resilience and boosting of self-esteem.</li>
<li>Reach out to family and friends, especially in times of crisis, that can help through rough spells.</li>
<li>Get treatment at the earliest sign of a problem to help prevent depression from worsening.</li>
<li>Consider getting long-term maintenance treatment to help prevent a relapse of symptoms.</li>
</ul>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id=""><span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"></span></h1></div>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>2.5.7 Types of depression</p>
<p>2.5.7.1 Postpartum Depression</p>
<p>Preamble</p>
<p>Postpartum depression can be moderate to severe depression in a woman after she has given birth. It may occur soon after delivery or up to a year later. However, the most common occurrence is within the first three months after delivery.</p>
<p>2.5.7.2 Causes of Postpartum depression</p>
<p>The exact causes of postpartum depression are unknown. However, changes in hormone levels during and after pregnancy may affect a woman’s mood. Many non-hormonal factors may also affect mood during this period. These include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Changes in the body during pregnancy and delivery</li>
<li>Physical health problems during pregnancy and delivery e.g. urinary incontinence</li>
<li>History of depression</li>
<li>Changes in work and social relationships</li>
<li>Limited social support</li>
<li>Inadequate sleep</li>
<li>Anxious about your ability to be a good mother</li>
</ul>
<p>2.5.7.3 Risk factors of Postpartum Depression</p>
<ul>
<li>A woman may have higher chances of postpartum depression because she is under 20 years of age.</li>
<li>Currently abuses alcohol, takes illegal substances, or smokes (these also cause serious health risks for the baby).</li>
<li>Did not plan the pregnancy, or had mixed feelings about the pregnancy</li>
<li>Had depression, bipolar or an anxiety disorder before the pregnancy, or with a past pregnancy.</li>
<li>Had a stressful event during the pregnancy or delivery, including personal illness, death or illness of a loved one, a difficult or emergency delivery, premature delivery, or illness or birth defect in the baby.</li>
<li>Have a close family member who has had depression or anxiety.</li>
<li>Have a poor relationship with their significant other or are single.</li>
<li>Have money or housing problems.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Have little support from family, friends, or your spouse or partner.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-5-8-management-of-postpartum-depression">2.5.8 Management of Postpartum Depression<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-5-8-management-of-postpartum-depression" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A new mother who has any symptoms of postpartum depression should contact her doctor or nurse right away to get help.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="psychosocial-interventions">PSYCHOSOCIAL INTERVENTIONS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#psychosocial-interventions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>i. Psychoeducation: Key messages to the person and the carers</p>
<ul>
<li>Depression is a very common condition that can happen to anybody.</li>
<li>The occurrence of depression does not mean that the person is weak or lazy.</li>
<li>Negative attitudes of others (e.g. “You should be stronger”, “Pull yourself together”) may be because depression is not a visible condition, unlike a fracture or a wound.</li>
<li>There is also the misconception that people with depression can easily control their symptoms by sheer willpower.</li>
<li>People with depression tend to have unrealistically negative opinions about themselves, their life and their future.</li>
<li>Their current situation may be very difficult, but depression can cause unjustified thoughts of hopelessness and worthlessness.</li>
<li>These views are likely to improve once the depression improves. Thoughts of self-harm or suicide are common. If they notice these thoughts, they should not act on them, but should tell a trusted person and come</li>
</ul>
<p>ii. Reduce stress and strengthen social support</p>
<ul>
<li>Assess for and try to reduce stressors.</li>
<li>Reactivate the person’s previous social network. Identify prior social activities that, if started again, may potentially provide direct or indirect psychosocial support, e.g. family gatherings, visiting neighbours, and community activities.</li>
</ul>
<p>This document is available on
© studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Joining a support group</p>
<p>iii. Promote functioning in daily activities and community life</p>
<p>Even if it is difficult, encourage the person to try to do as many of the following as possible:</p>
<ul>
<li>Try to start again (or continue) activities that were previously pleasurable.</li>
<li>Try to maintain regular sleeping and waking times. Also try to maintain sleep hygiene</li>
<li>Try to be as physically active as possible.</li>
<li>Try to eat regularly despite changes in appetite.</li>
<li>Try to spend time with trusted friends and family.</li>
<li>Try to participate in community and other social activities as much as possible.</li>
<li>Explain to the person and carer that these activities can all help improve mood.</li>
</ul>
<p>iv. Brief psychological management for depression</p>
<p>Some of the intervention recommended by WHO include;</p>
<ul>
<li>Problem management+ (PM+)</li>
<li>Self help+</li>
<li>Interpersonal psychotherapy (IPT)</li>
<li>Healthy Thinking – that describes management of perinatal depression.</li>
</ul>
<p>2.5.9 Prevention of postpartum depression</p>
<p>Here are some other helpful tips to the new mother:</p>
<ul>
<li>Ask your partner, family, and friends for help with the baby's needs and in the home.</li>
<li>Don't hide your feelings. Talk about them with your partner, family, and friends.</li>
<li>Don't make any major life changes during pregnancy or right after giving birth.</li>
<li>Don't try to do too much, or to be perfect.</li>
<li>Make time to go out, visit friends, or spend time alone with your partner.</li>
<li>Rest as much as you can. Sleep when the baby is sleeping.</li>
</ul>
<p>Talk with other mothers or join a support group.</p>
<p>Prevention</p>
<p>Preventive approaches are needed because of the long term effects of maternal Postpartum depression</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Psychosocial and psychological interventions</li>
<li>In-hospital psychological debriefings</li>
<li>Antenatal and postnatal classes</li>
<li>Home visits</li>
<li>Continuity of care and early postpartum follow-ups</li>
<li>Professional support provided postnatally</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="major-depression-disorder">Major Depression Disorder<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#major-depression-disorder" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Major depressive disorder is also called major depression. In this disorder individuals experience episodes of sad mood or anhedonia (loss of interest or pleasure) that last longer than two weeks and significantly disrupt their everyday function. Individuals may also have thoughts of self-harm. Some with this disorder may experience a single episode of severe depression in their lifetimes. For many others, recurrent episodes of such depression will occur throughout their lives.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="causes">Causes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#causes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Genetic – depressive disorders can be inherited from one or both parents</li>
<li>Biological factors- exposure to severe or long lasting stress can change the balance of chemicals in the brain that control brain.</li>
<li>Environmental factors- families with poor coping abilities, low levels of communication within the family and inconsistent response have a higher chance of developing MDD</li>
<li>Psychological factors – stress in childhood and adulthood like isolation and lack of support can lead to MDD</li>
</ul>
<p>Major depressive disorders may also be triggered by:</p>
<ul>
<li>Alcohol or drug use</li>
<li>Certain medical conditions, such as cancer or hypothyroidism</li>
<li>Particular types of medications including steroids</li>
<li>Abuse during childhood</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="symptoms">Symptoms<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#symptoms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Depressed mood- for children and adolescents this can also be an irritable mood</li>
</ul>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id=""><span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"></span></h1></div>
<p>studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Loss of interest or pleasure which have been present during the same 2-week period</li>
<li>Appetite disturbance – weight loss or gain</li>
</ul>
<p>The symptoms cause clinically significant distress or impairment in social occupational or other important area.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="management">Management<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="psychotherapy">Psychotherapy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#psychotherapy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Psychotherapy or talk therapy is common term for treating depression by talking about your condition and related issues with a mental therapy health professional.</p>
<ul>
<li>Cognitive behavioral therapy or interpersonal therapy are forms that are also used.</li>
<li>Electroconvulsive therapy is used in depression to impact the function and effect of neurotransmitter in the brain</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="medication">Medication<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#medication" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="persistent-depressive-disorder">Persistent Depressive disorder<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#persistent-depressive-disorder" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="definition">Definition<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#definition" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<p>Persistent depressive disorder is a form of chronic (ongoing depression). Although symptoms of persistent depressive disorder may be less severe than other types of depression, they are longer-lasting in duration.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="symptoms">Symptoms<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#symptoms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<p>Symptoms of persistent depressive disorder can cause significant impairment and may include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Feelings of hopelessness</li>
<li>Low self-esteem</li>
<li>Low energy or fatigue</li>
<li>Poor appetite or overeating</li>
<li>Difficulty with concentration and decision-making</li>
<li>Difficulty falling/staying asleep (insomnia) or sleeping too much (hypersomnia)</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="risk-factors">Risk factors<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#risk-factors" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>The exact cause of persistent depressive disorder is unknown. As with major depression, it may involve more than one cause, such as:</p>
<ul>
<li>Biological differences. People with persistent depressive disorder may have physical changes in their brains. The significance of these changes is still uncertain, but they may eventually help pinpoint causes.</li>
<li>Brain chemistry. Exposure to severe or long lasting stress can change the balance of chemicals in the brain that control mood</li>
<li>Heredity- Persistent depressive disorder appears to be more common in people whose blood relatives also have the condition.</li>
<li>Life events- experiencing a trauma or hardships may trigger the symptoms in people who have how an inherited susceptibility to develop</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="management">Management<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The two primary methods for treating persistent depressive disorder include:</p>
<ol>
<li>Pharmaco-therapy- antidepressant medication and</li>
<li>Psychotherapy or talk therapy.</li>
</ol>
<p>The combination of both longer term treatment methods may prove to be most effective.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="premenstrual-dysphoric-disorder">Premenstrual Dysphoric Disorder<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#premenstrual-dysphoric-disorder" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="definition">Definition<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#definition" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Premenstrual dysphoric disorder (PMDD) is a severe form of depression disorder that includes physical and behavioural symptoms that usually resolve with the onset of menstruation.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="risk-factors">Risk factors<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#risk-factors" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Genetic and physiological</li>
<li>Environmental</li>
<li>Hormones</li>
</ul>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-6-0-epilepsy">2.6.0 Epilepsy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-6-0-epilepsy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="2-6-1-purpose">2.6.1 Purpose<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-6-1-purpose" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>The purpose of this unit is to equip the community health personnel with knowledge and skills to enable them create awareness, identify, demystify and assist in management, effective referral and follow up of epilepsy.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="2-6-2-definition-of-epilepsy">2.6.2 Definition of epilepsy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-6-2-definition-of-epilepsy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Epilepsy is a chronic brain disorder characterized by repetitive, sudden onset, short-lived seizures occurring more than twice in a year, that may arise from many and varied causes. Other commonly used terms for epilepsy are Convulsions; Seizures; Fits; Attack; Kifafa (Kiswahili).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="2-6-3-common-myths-and-misconceptions-associated-with-epilepsy">2.6.3 Common myths and misconceptions associated with epilepsy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-6-3-common-myths-and-misconceptions-associated-with-epilepsy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Table 6: Myths &amp; Facts about Epilepsy</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Myth / Misconception</th><th>Fact</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Epilepsy is contagious</td><td>You cannot get epilepsy from another person</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>You can swallow your tongue during a seizure</th><th>It is physically impossible to swallow your tongue. Do not force something into the mouth of someone having a seizure. Doing so may result in chipped teeth, injured gums, or broken jaws.</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Epilepsy is a product of witchcraft</td><td>Epilepsy is NOT a product of witchcraft or any superstitious activity. It is a seizure disorder.</td></tr><tr><td>Epilepsy is a permanent disorder</td><td>Epilepsy is NOT a permanent disorder. Most cases would manifest during childhood but seizures would stop at certain age with treatment.</td></tr><tr><td>People with epilepsy look different</td><td>People with epilepsy look like anyone else</td></tr><tr><td>Epilepsy is a curse</td><td>Epilepsy is not a curse but a mental health challenge that can be managed.</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-6-4-signs-and-symptoms-of-epilepsy">2.6.4 Signs and Symptoms of Epilepsy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-6-4-signs-and-symptoms-of-epilepsy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Epilepsy is caused by abnormal activity in the brain, seizures can affect any process coordinated by the brain and can thus lead to:</p>
<ul>
<li>A staring spell</li>
<li>Temporary confusion</li>
<li>Involuntary jerking movements of the arms and legs</li>
<li>Loss of consciousness and awareness.</li>
</ul>
<p>Symptoms vary depending on the type of seizure. In most cases, a person with epilepsy will tend to have the same type of seizure each time, so the symptoms will be similar from one episode to another.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-6-5-classification-of-epilepsy">2.6.5 Classification of epilepsy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-6-5-classification-of-epilepsy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Epilepsy can be classified into two major types:</p>
<ol>
<li>Partial epilepsy: Seizures that result from abnormal activity in just one part of the brain with either impairment or retention of consciousness.</li>
<li>Generalized epilepsy: Seizures that result from a widespread involvement of large parts of the brain simultaneously leading to loss of consciousness.</li>
</ol>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>s</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>2.6.6 Causes of Epilepsy</p>
<p>Some of the common causes of epilepsy include:</p>
<ol>
<li>Brain Infections</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Meningitis</li>
<li>Cerebral malaria</li>
<li>HIV-AIDS – opportunistic infections</li>
</ul>
<ol start="2">
<li>Trauma</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Head injury such as road traffic injuries and assault</li>
<li>Birth trauma, especially common in home deliveries where there is prolonged labour.</li>
</ul>
<ol start="3">
<li>Lack of oxygen to the brain</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Lack of oxygen to the baby’s brain at birth (birth asphyxia).</li>
</ul>
<ol start="4">
<li>Structural brain problems</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Accumulation of fluid in the baby’s head</li>
<li>Tumours/growths in the head</li>
<li>Abscesses - pus accumulation in the brain.</li>
</ul>
<p>NOTE: There are other unknown causes of epilepsy</p>
<ol start="5">
<li>Genetic: Runs in families – team add more content</li>
</ol>
<p>2.6.7 Triggering Factors of Epilepsy</p>
<p>People with Epilepsy are likely to develop seizures when exposed to certain situations or conditions. These include;</p>
<ul>
<li>Non-adherence to treatment</li>
<li>Sleep deprivation</li>
<li>Infections,</li>
<li>Flickering of lights</li>
<li>Drug and Substance abuse</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Emotional Stress</li>
<li>Excessive physical exercise.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-6-8-the-do-s-and-don-ts-during-an-epileptic-seizure">2.6.8 The Do's and Don'ts during an epileptic seizure<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-6-8-the-do-s-and-don-ts-during-an-epileptic-seizure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Table 7: Do's &amp; Don'ts</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Dos</th><th>DONTs</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>• Move patient away from danger such as fire, traffic or water</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Take away any objects that could harm the patient</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Loosen tight clothes, remove glasses</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Turn the patient on their left side, recovery position</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Put something soft under the head</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Remain with the patient until he or she regains consciousness</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Allow rest and then resumption of activity the patient was doing, if he feels like it.</td><td>• Do NOT try to put anything into the mouth</td></tr><tr><td>• Do NOT give anything to drink</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Do NOT try to stop the jerking, or restrain the movements.</td><td></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Unconscious patients should be placed in this recovery position to minimize the risk of them choking in case they vomit. The recovery position refers to one of a series of variations on a lateral recumbent or three-quarters prone position of the body, into which an unconscious but breathing client can be placed as part of first aid treatment.</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-6-9-principles-of-management-of-epilepsy">2.6.9 Principles of management of epilepsy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-6-9-principles-of-management-of-epilepsy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Community awareness to demystify epilepsy and reduce stigma in order to facilitate early health seeking by the patients</li>
<li>Teach those close to the patient on what to do in the event of a seizure</li>
<li>Prompt referral and follow-up of persons with signs of epilepsy for confirmation of diagnosis</li>
<li>Strict compliance and adherence to the treatment regimen prescribed.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-6-10-principles-of-prevention-of-epilepsy">2.6.10 Principles of prevention of epilepsy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-6-10-principles-of-prevention-of-epilepsy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The following measures should be considered in the prevention of epilepsy:</p>
<ul>
<li>Encourage and support mothers to deliver in health facilities to avoid birth trauma</li>
<li>Seek treatment promptly to avoid complications of diseases such as meningitis and severe malaria</li>
<li>Prevention of infectious diseases e.g. malaria, meningitis, HIV/AIDS.</li>
<li>Encourage and support mothers to ensure their children complete immunization schedule</li>
<li>prevention of road traffic accidents and other courses of trauma</li>
<li>Prompt management of fever in children to avoid febrile convulsions.</li>
<li>Strict adherence to prescribed medication.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-6-11-role-of-health-care-workers-in-management-of-epilepsy">2.6.11 Role of Health Care Workers in Management of Epilepsy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-6-11-role-of-health-care-workers-in-management-of-epilepsy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Create awareness on epilepsy to the community members so as to:</p>
<ul>
<li>Reduce stigmatization of persons</li>
<li>Demystify the common myths and misconceptions</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Conduct regular home visits for early identification of cases in the community.</li>
<li>Make referrals of clients with epilepsy and act as link person between patients and the health facilities</li>
<li>Conduct follow-up visits to client to monitor their drug adherence</li>
<li>Maintain updated clients' data/records.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-7-schizophrenia-and-psychoses">2.7 Schizophrenia and Psychoses<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-7-schizophrenia-and-psychoses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="2-7-1-purpose">2.7.1 Purpose<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-7-1-purpose" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>The purpose of this unit is to equip healthcare workers with knowledge, skills and attitudes to enable them create awareness, identify, demystify and assist in management, effective referral and follow up of schizophrenia and psychotic cases.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="2-7-2-definition-of-schizophrenia">2.7.2 Definition of Schizophrenia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-7-2-definition-of-schizophrenia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Schizophrenia is a chronic and severe mental disorder characterized by distortions in thinking, perception, emotions, language, sense of self and behaviour. Common experiences include hallucinations (hearing voices or seeing things that are not there) and delusions (fixed, false beliefs).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="2-7-3-causes-of-schizophrenia">2.7.3 Causes of Schizophrenia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-7-3-causes-of-schizophrenia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>It's not known what causes schizophrenia, but researchers believe that a combination</p>
<ul>
<li>Of genetics, brain chemistry and environment contribute to development of the disorder.</li>
<li>Problems with certain naturally occurring brain chemicals, including neurotransmitters called dopamine and glutamate, may contribute to schizophrenia.</li>
<li>Neuroimaging studies show differences in the brain structure and central nervous system of people with schizophrenia indicating that schizophrenia is a brain disease.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="2-7-4-risk-factors-associated-with-schizophrenia">2.7.4 Risk factors Associated with Schizophrenia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-7-4-risk-factors-associated-with-schizophrenia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Although the precise cause of schizophrenia isn't known, certain factors seem to increase the risk of developing or triggering schizophrenia, including:</p>
<ul>
<li>Having a family history of schizophrenia</li>
</ul>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id=""><span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"></span></h1></div>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Some pregnancy and birth complications, such as malnutrition or exposure to toxins or viruses that may impact brain development</li>
<li>Taking mind-altering (psychoactive or psychotropic) drugs during teen years and young adulthood</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-7-5-symptoms-of-schizophrenia">2.7.5 Symptoms of Schizophrenia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-7-5-symptoms-of-schizophrenia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Schizophrenia involves a range of problems with thinking (cognition), behavior and emotions. Signs and symptoms may vary, but usually involve delusions, hallucinations or disorganized speech, and reflect an impaired ability to function.</p>
<ol>
<li>Delusions. These are false beliefs that are not based in reality. For example,</li>
</ol>
<p>One think that they are being harmed or harassed; certain gestures or comments are directed at them;</p>
<p>One feeling that they have exceptional ability or fame or another person is in love with them; or a major catastrophe is about to occur.</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>Hallucinations. These usually involve seeing, feeling things that are not there or hearing things that don't exist.</li>
</ol>
<p>Hallucinations can be in any of the five senses, but hearing voices is the most common hallucination.</p>
<ol start="3">
<li>Disorganized thinking (speech). Disorganized thinking is inferred from disorganized speech, incoherent or irrelevant speech. Effective communication can be impaired, and answers to questions may be partially or completely unrelated.</li>
</ol>
<p>Rarely, speech may include putting together meaningless words that can't be understood, sometimes known as word salad.</p>
<ol start="4">
<li>Extremely disorganized or abnormal motor behavior. This may show in a number of ways, from childlike silliness to unpredictable agitation.</li>
</ol>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Behavior isn't focused on a goal, so it's hard to do tasks. Behavior can include resistance to instructions, inappropriate or bizarre posture, a complete lack of response, or useless and excessive movement.</p>
<p>Abnormal behaviour: disorganized behaviour such as wandering aimlessly, mumbling or laughing to self, strange appearance, self-neglect or appearing unkempt.</p>
<ol start="5">
<li>Negative symptoms. This refers to reduced or lack of ability to function normally.</li>
</ol>
<p>For example, the person may neglect personal hygiene or appear to lack emotion (doesn't make eye contact, doesn't change facial expressions or speaks in a monotone).</p>
<p>Also, the person may lose interest in everyday activities, socially withdraw or lack the ability to experience pleasure.</p>
<ol start="6">
<li>Positive symptoms: This refers to symptoms that are excess or added to normal mental functioning. Feelings or behaviors that are usually not present, such as believing that what other people are saying is not true (delusions); hearing, seeing, tasting, feeling, or smelling things that others do not experience (hallucinations); disorganized speech and behavior.</li>
</ol>
<p>Note:
Also, recreational substance use, such as marijuana, methamphetamines or LSD, can sometimes cause similar signs and symptoms.</p>
<p>Compared with schizophrenia symptoms in adults, teens may be:</p>
<ul>
<li>Less likely to have delusions</li>
</ul>
<p>More likely to have visual hallucinations</p>
<p>Symptoms can vary in type and severity over time, with periods of worsening and remission of symptoms. Some symptoms may always be present. Schizophrenia symptoms typically start in the early to mid-20s. It is uncommon for children to be diagnosed with schizophrenia and rare for those older than age 45.</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id=""><span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"></span></h1></div>
<p>studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="symptoms-in-special-populations-teenagers">Symptoms in Special Populations (teenagers)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#symptoms-in-special-populations-teenagers" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Schizophrenia symptoms in teenagers are similar to those in adults, but the condition may be more difficult to recognize. This may be in part because some of the early symptoms of schizophrenia in teenagers are common for typical development during teen years, such as:</p>
<ul>
<li>Withdrawal from friends and family</li>
<li>A drop in performance at school</li>
<li>Trouble sleeping</li>
<li>Irritability or depressed mood</li>
<li>Lack of motivation</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-7-6-prevention-of-schizophrenia">2.7.6 Prevention of schizophrenia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-7-6-prevention-of-schizophrenia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Prevention of schizophrenia is difficult as there are no reliable markers for the later development of the disorder. There is no sure way to prevent schizophrenia, but sticking with the treatment plan can help prevent relapses or worsening of symptoms. In addition, researchers hope that learning more about risk factors for schizophrenia may lead to earlier diagnosis and treatment.</p>
<p>However, there is tentative evidence for the effectiveness of the following measures:</p>
<ul>
<li>Avoid drugs that have been associated with development of the disorder, including cannabis, cocaine, and amphetamines</li>
<li>Cognitive behavioral therapy may reduce the risk of psychoses in those at high risk after a year</li>
<li>Early treatment for those experiencing symptoms of psychoses for the first time</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-7-7-management-of-schizophrenia">2.7.7 Management of Schizophrenia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-7-7-management-of-schizophrenia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="2-7-7-1-psychosocial-interventions">2.7.7.1 Psychosocial interventions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-7-7-1-psychosocial-interventions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Once psychoses recedes, in addition to continuing on medication, psychological and social (psychosocial) interventions are important. These may include:</p>
<ul>
<li><strong>Individual therapy.</strong> Psychotherapy may help to normalize thought patterns. Also, learning to cope with stress and identify early warning signs of relapse can help people with schizophrenia manage their illness.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Social and life skills training. This focuses on improving communication and social interactions and improving the ability to participate in daily activities.</li>
<li>Family therapy. This provides support and education to families dealing with schizophrenia and educating the public to decrease stigma and discrimination.</li>
<li>Vocational rehabilitation and supported employment. This focuses on helping people with schizophrenia prepare for, find and keep jobs facilitating independent living.</li>
<li>During crisis periods or times of severe symptoms, refer to the mental health professional</li>
<li>A case manager or someone on the treatment team can help find resources. With appropriate treatment, most people with schizophrenia can manage their illness.</li>
</ul>
<p>Note:
Most individuals with schizophrenia require some form of daily living support. Many communities have programs to help people with schizophrenia get jobs, housing, join self-help groups and manage crises situations.</p>
<p>2.7.7.2 Electroconvulsive Therapy
For adults with schizophrenia who do not respond to drug therapy, electroconvulsive therapy (ECT) may be considered.</p>
<p>2.7.7.3 Refer for Medical Management
During crisis periods or times of severe symptoms, refer the patient a mental health professional.</p>
<p>2.7.7.4 Prevention of Schizophrenia
There is no sure way to prevent schizophrenia, but sticking with the treatment plan can help prevent relapses or worsening of symptoms. In addition, researchers hope that learning more about risk factors for schizophrenia may lead to earlier diagnosis and treatment.</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="2-8-dementia">2.8 Dementia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-8-dementia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="2-8-1-purpose">2.8.1 Purpose<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-8-1-purpose" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The purpose of this unit is to equip healthcare workers with knowledge, skills and attitudes to enable them create awareness, identify, demystify and assist in management, effective referral and follow up of Dementia</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="2-8-2-definition-of-dementia">2.8.2 Definition of dementia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-8-2-definition-of-dementia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Dementia is a general term for loss of memory, language, problem-solving and other thinking abilities that are severe enough to interfere with daily life. Dementia is not a single disease; it's an overall term — like heart disease — that covers a wide range of specific medical conditions, including Alzheimer's disease.</p>
<p>Disorders grouped under the general term "dementia" are caused by abnormal brain changes. These changes trigger a decline in thinking skills, also known as cognitive abilities, severe enough to impair daily life and independent function. They also affect behavior, feelings and relationships. Dementia is not part of normal ageing. Although it can occur at any age, it's more common in older people.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260110-d95bf64e.jpeg" alt="img-3.jpeg">
Figure 3: Dementia</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="2-8-3-types-of-dementia">2.8.3 Types of dementia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-8-3-types-of-dementia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Table 8: Dementia types</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Type</th><th>Explanation</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Alzheimer's disease</td><td>Alzheimer’s is a degenerative brain disease that is caused by complex brain changes following cell damage. It leads to dementia symptoms that gradually worsen over time. . It accounts for 60-80% of cases.</td></tr><tr><td>Vascular dementia</td><td>It occurs because of microscopic bleeding and blood vessel blockage in the brain, is the second most common cause of dementia.</td></tr><tr><td>Lewy body dementia (LBD)</td><td>is the third most common cause of dementia after Alzheimer's disease and vascular dementia, accounting for 5 to 10 percent of cases. It is a type of progressive dementia that leads to a decline in thinking, reasoning and independent function because of abnormal microscopic deposits that damage brain cells over time</td></tr><tr><td>Frontotemporal dementia (FTD) or frontotemporal degenerations</td><td>Refers to a group of disorders caused by progressive nerve cell loss in the brain's frontal lobes (the areas behind your forehead) or its temporal lobes (the regions behind your ears). The nerve cell damage caused by frontotemporal dementia leads to loss of function in these brain regions, which variably cause deterioration in behavior, personality and/or difficulty with producing or comprehending language.</td></tr><tr><td>HIV-associated dementia</td><td>Occurs when the HIV virus spreads to the brain. Symptoms of HIV-associated dementia include loss of memory, difficulty thinking, concentrating, and or speaking clearly, lack of interest in activities and gradual loss of motor skills.</td></tr><tr><td>Parkinson's disease dementia</td><td>Is a decline in thinking and reasoning that develops in many people living with Parkinson’s at least a year after diagnosis. The brain changes caused by Parkinson’s disease begin in a region that plays a key role in movement, leading to early symptoms that include tremors and shakiness, muscle stiffness, a shuffling step, stooped posture, difficulty initiating movement and lack of facial</td></tr></tbody></table>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="2-8-4-risk-factors-for-dementia">2.8.4 Risk factors for dementia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-8-4-risk-factors-for-dementia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Risk factors include age, genetics, lifestyle choices and health conditions</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="2-8-5-causes-of-dementia">2.8.5 Causes of dementia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-8-5-causes-of-dementia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Dementia is caused by damage to or loss of nerve cells and their connections in the brain. Depending on the area of the brain that's affected by the damage, dementia can affect people differently and cause different symptoms. There can be reversible or non-reversible causes.</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Dementias are often grouped by what they have in common, such as the protein or proteins deposited in the brain or the part of the brain that's affected. Some diseases present like dementias, such as those caused by a reaction to medication or vitamin deficiencies, and they might improve with treatment.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-8-6-signs-and-symptoms-of-dementia">2.8.6 Signs and symptoms of dementia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-8-6-signs-and-symptoms-of-dementia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Table 11: Common signs and symptoms of dementia:</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Cognitive changes</th><th>Psychological and behavioral changes</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>• Problems with short-term memory loss, which is usually noticed by a spouse or someone else</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Difficulty communicating or finding words</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Difficulty with visual and spatial abilities, such as getting lost while driving</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Difficulty reasoning or problem-solving</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Difficulty handling complex tasks</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Difficulty with planning and organizing</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Difficulty with coordination and motor functions</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Confusion and disorientation</td><td>• Personality changes</td></tr><tr><td>• Depression</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Anxiety</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Inappropriate behavior</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Paranoia (a thought process that causes you to have an irrational suspicion or mistrust of others)</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Agitation</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Hallucination (sensory experiences that appear real but are created by one’s mind)</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>• Getting lost (wandering)</td><td></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-8-7-management-of-dementia">2.8.7 Management of dementia<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-8-7-management-of-dementia" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>If dementia is suspected, individuals should be referred to a health facility to be evaluated and a diagnosis made. Both non-pharmacologic and pharmacologic interventions are needed to optimally manage the cognitive, behavioral and psychological symptoms of dementia.</p>
<p>Below we focus on the management process:</p>
